Practice Problems

Lecture examples are clearly marked. All others are original problems generated for extra practice.

VOP with nonconstant coefficients
New · 1 \(t^2 y'' - 2ty' + 2y = t^3,\quad y_1 = t,\; y_2 = t^2\) VOP, given \(y_1, y_2\) ▾
1
Standard form — divide by \(t^2\)
\(a(t) = t^2\), so divide: \(f(t)/a(t) = t^3/t^2 = t\).
\[ y'' - \frac{2}{t}y' + \frac{2}{t^2}y = t \]
2
Wronskian
\[ {\color{#8b9eca}{W}} = \begin{vmatrix}{\color{#e8b86d}{t}} & {\color{#7eb8a4}{t^2}} \\ {\color{#e8b86d}{1}} & {\color{#7eb8a4}{2t}}\end{vmatrix} = 2t^2 - t^2 = {\color{#8b9eca}{t^2}} \]
3
Compute \(u_1'\) and \(u_2'\)
\[ u_1' = \frac{-{\color{#7eb8a4}{t^2}}\cdot t}{{\color{#8b9eca}{t^2}}} = \frac{-t^3}{t^2} = -t \;\Rightarrow\; {\color{#e8b86d}{u_1 = -\tfrac{t^2}{2}}} \]
\[ u_2' = \frac{{\color{#e8b86d}{t}}\cdot t}{{\color{#8b9eca}{t^2}}} = \frac{t^2}{t^2} = 1 \;\Rightarrow\; {\color{#7eb8a4}{u_2 = t}} \]
4
Assemble \(y_p\)
\[ y_p = {\color{#e8b86d}{-\tfrac{t^2}{2}}}\cdot{\color{#e8b86d}{t}} + {\color{#7eb8a4}{t}}\cdot{\color{#7eb8a4}{t^2}} = -\tfrac{t^3}{2} + t^3 = \tfrac{t^3}{2} \]
General solution
\[ y = c_1 t + c_2 t^2 + \tfrac{t^3}{2} \]
Systems in matrix form
New · 2 Write \(y'' - 5y' + 6y = 0\) as a first-order system \(\mathbf{x}' = A\mathbf{x}\) Single ODE → system ▾
1
Introduce state variables
Let \({\color{#e8b86d}{x_1 = y}}\) and \({\color{#7eb8a4}{x_2 = y'}}\). Then \(x_1' = y' = x_2\) and \(x_2' = y'' = 5y' - 6y = 5x_2 - 6x_1\).
2
Matrix form
\[ \begin{bmatrix}{\color{#e8b86d}{x_1}}\\{\color{#7eb8a4}{x_2}}\end{bmatrix}' = {\color{#8b9eca}{\begin{bmatrix}0 & 1 \\ -6 & 5\end{bmatrix}}}\begin{bmatrix}{\color{#e8b86d}{x_1}}\\{\color{#7eb8a4}{x_2}}\end{bmatrix} \]
Eigenvalues of \(A\): \(r^2 - 5r + 6 = (r-2)(r-3) = 0\) → \(r = 2, 3\). These match the characteristic roots of the original ODE — as expected.
Answer
\[ A = {\color{#8b9eca}{\begin{bmatrix}0 & 1 \\ -6 & 5\end{bmatrix}}}, \qquad \text{eigenvalues } \lambda = 2, 3 \]
Complex eigenvalue systems
New · 3 \(x_1' = x_2,\quad x_2' = -4x_1\) Pure imaginary eigenvalues ▾
1
Matrix and eigenvalues
\[ {\color{#8b9eca}{A}} = \begin{bmatrix}0 & 1 \\ -4 & 0\end{bmatrix}, \quad \det(A - \lambda I) = \lambda^2 + 4 = 0 \;\Rightarrow\; {\color{#c47b7b}{\lambda = \pm 2i}} \]
So \({\color{#c47b7b}{\alpha = 0}}\) and \({\color{#c47b7b}{\beta = 2}}\). Work with \(\lambda = 2i\).
2
Find eigenvector for \(\lambda = 2i\)
\[ A - 2iI = \begin{bmatrix}-2i & 1 \\ -4 & -2i\end{bmatrix} \]
First row: \(-2i\,y_1 + y_2 = 0\). Pick \(y_1 = 1\) → \(y_2 = 2i\).
\[ \text{Eigenvector: }\begin{bmatrix}1\\2i\end{bmatrix} = \underbrace{\begin{bmatrix}1\\0\end{bmatrix}}_{{\color{#e8b86d}{\mathbf{a}}}} + \underbrace{\begin{bmatrix}0\\2\end{bmatrix}}_{{\color{#7eb8a4}{\mathbf{b}}}}i \]
3
Apply \(w_1, w_2\) formulas — note \(\alpha=0\) so no exponential decay
\[ {\color{#e8b86d}{w_1}} = e^{{\color{#c47b7b}{0\cdot t}}}\!\left[\cos({\color{#c47b7b}{2}}t)\begin{bmatrix}{\color{#e8b86d}{1}}\\{\color{#e8b86d}{0}}\end{bmatrix} - \sin({\color{#c47b7b}{2}}t)\begin{bmatrix}{\color{#7eb8a4}{0}}\\{\color{#7eb8a4}{2}}\end{bmatrix}\right] = \begin{bmatrix}\cos 2t \\ -2\sin 2t\end{bmatrix} \]
\[ {\color{#7eb8a4}{w_2}} = \left[\sin({\color{#c47b7b}{2}}t)\begin{bmatrix}{\color{#e8b86d}{1}}\\{\color{#e8b86d}{0}}\end{bmatrix} + \cos({\color{#c47b7b}{2}}t)\begin{bmatrix}{\color{#7eb8a4}{0}}\\{\color{#7eb8a4}{2}}\end{bmatrix}\right] = \begin{bmatrix}\sin 2t \\ 2\cos 2t\end{bmatrix} \]
General solution
\[ \mathbf{x}(t) = c_1\begin{bmatrix}\cos 2t \\ -2\sin 2t\end{bmatrix} + c_2\begin{bmatrix}\sin 2t \\ 2\cos 2t\end{bmatrix} \]
Note: \(\alpha=0\) means oscillation with no decay — this is the vector-system analogue of \(y''+4y=0\).
New · 4 \(\mathbf{x}' = \begin{bmatrix}2 & -5 \\ 1 & -2\end{bmatrix}\mathbf{x}\) Pure imaginary — verify \(\alpha = 0\) ▾
1
Eigenvalues
\[ \det(A-\lambda I) = (2-\lambda)(-2-\lambda)+5 = -4+\lambda^2+5 = \lambda^2+1 = 0 \;\Rightarrow\; {\color{#c47b7b}{\lambda = \pm i}} \]
\({\color{#c47b7b}{\alpha=0}},\; {\color{#c47b7b}{\beta=1}}\). Work with \(\lambda = i\).
2
Eigenvector for \(\lambda = i\)
\[ A - iI = \begin{bmatrix}2-i & -5 \\ 1 & -2-i\end{bmatrix} \]
First row: \((2-i)y_1 - 5y_2 = 0\). Pick \(y_1 = 5\) → \(y_2 = 2-i\).
\[ \text{Eigenvector: }\begin{bmatrix}5\\2-i\end{bmatrix} = \underbrace{\begin{bmatrix}5\\2\end{bmatrix}}_{{\color{#e8b86d}{\mathbf{a}}}} + \underbrace{\begin{bmatrix}0\\-1\end{bmatrix}}_{{\color{#7eb8a4}{\mathbf{b}}}}i \]
3
Apply \(w_1, w_2\) formulas
\[ {\color{#e8b86d}{w_1}} = \cos t\begin{bmatrix}{\color{#e8b86d}{5}}\\{\color{#e8b86d}{2}}\end{bmatrix} - \sin t\begin{bmatrix}{\color{#7eb8a4}{0}}\\{\color{#7eb8a4}{-1}}\end{bmatrix} = \begin{bmatrix}5\cos t \\ 2\cos t + \sin t\end{bmatrix} \]
\[ {\color{#7eb8a4}{w_2}} = \sin t\begin{bmatrix}{\color{#e8b86d}{5}}\\{\color{#e8b86d}{2}}\end{bmatrix} + \cos t\begin{bmatrix}{\color{#7eb8a4}{0}}\\{\color{#7eb8a4}{-1}}\end{bmatrix} = \begin{bmatrix}5\sin t \\ 2\sin t - \cos t\end{bmatrix} \]
General solution
\[ \mathbf{x}(t) = c_1\begin{bmatrix}5\cos t \\ 2\cos t + \sin t\end{bmatrix} + c_2\begin{bmatrix}5\sin t \\ 2\sin t - \cos t\end{bmatrix} \]
New · 5 \(\mathbf{x}' = \begin{bmatrix}1 & -2 \\ 4 & 1\end{bmatrix}\mathbf{x}\) Complex with nonzero real part \(\alpha \neq 0\) ▾
1
Eigenvalues
\[ \det(A-\lambda I) = (1-\lambda)^2 + 8 = 0 \;\Rightarrow\; (1-\lambda)^2 = -8 \;\Rightarrow\; {\color{#c47b7b}{\lambda = 1 \pm 2\sqrt{2}\,i}} \]
\({\color{#c47b7b}{\alpha = 1}},\; {\color{#c47b7b}{\beta = 2\sqrt{2}}}\). Since \(\alpha > 0\), solutions grow over time.
2
Eigenvector for \(\lambda = 1+2\sqrt{2}\,i\)
\[ A - (1+2\sqrt{2}\,i)I = \begin{bmatrix}-2\sqrt{2}\,i & -2 \\ 4 & -2\sqrt{2}\,i\end{bmatrix} \]
From first row: \(-2\sqrt{2}\,i\,y_1 - 2y_2 = 0\). Pick \(y_1 = 1\) → \(y_2 = -\sqrt{2}\,i\).
\[ \text{Eigenvector: }\begin{bmatrix}1\\-\sqrt{2}\,i\end{bmatrix} = \underbrace{\begin{bmatrix}1\\0\end{bmatrix}}_{{\color{#e8b86d}{\mathbf{a}}}} + \underbrace{\begin{bmatrix}0\\-\sqrt{2}\end{bmatrix}}_{{\color{#7eb8a4}{\mathbf{b}}}}i \]
3
Apply \(w_1, w_2\) with \(e^{\alpha t} = e^t\)
\[ {\color{#e8b86d}{w_1}} = e^{{\color{#c47b7b}{t}}}\!\left[\cos({\color{#c47b7b}{2\sqrt{2}}}t)\begin{bmatrix}{\color{#e8b86d}{1}}\\{\color{#e8b86d}{0}}\end{bmatrix} - \sin({\color{#c47b7b}{2\sqrt{2}}}t)\begin{bmatrix}{\color{#7eb8a4}{0}}\\{\color{#7eb8a4}{-\sqrt{2}}}\end{bmatrix}\right] = e^t\begin{bmatrix}\cos(2\sqrt{2}\,t) \\ \sqrt{2}\sin(2\sqrt{2}\,t)\end{bmatrix} \]
\[ {\color{#7eb8a4}{w_2}} = e^{{\color{#c47b7b}{t}}}\!\left[\sin({\color{#c47b7b}{2\sqrt{2}}}t)\begin{bmatrix}{\color{#e8b86d}{1}}\\{\color{#e8b86d}{0}}\end{bmatrix} + \cos({\color{#c47b7b}{2\sqrt{2}}}t)\begin{bmatrix}{\color{#7eb8a4}{0}}\\{\color{#7eb8a4}{-\sqrt{2}}}\end{bmatrix}\right] = e^t\begin{bmatrix}\sin(2\sqrt{2}\,t) \\ -\sqrt{2}\cos(2\sqrt{2}\,t)\end{bmatrix} \]
General solution
\[ \mathbf{x}(t) = c_1\, e^t\begin{bmatrix}\cos(2\sqrt{2}\,t) \\ \sqrt{2}\sin(2\sqrt{2}\,t)\end{bmatrix} + c_2\, e^t\begin{bmatrix}\sin(2\sqrt{2}\,t) \\ -\sqrt{2}\cos(2\sqrt{2}\,t)\end{bmatrix} \]
Since \(\alpha = 1 > 0\), both components grow exponentially while oscillating — an unstable spiral.
Homework Problems — §9.5
HW · 9.5 #12 \(\mathbf{x}' = \begin{bmatrix}1 & 3 \\ 12 & 1\end{bmatrix}\mathbf{x}\) Real distinct eigenvalues ▾
1
Characteristic equation
Compute \(\det(A - \lambda I) = 0\):
\[ \det\begin{bmatrix}1-\lambda & 3 \\ 12 & 1-\lambda\end{bmatrix} = (1-\lambda)^2 - 36 = 0 \] \[ \lambda^2 - 2\lambda + 1 - 36 = 0 \;\Rightarrow\; \lambda^2 - 2\lambda - 35 = 0 \;\Rightarrow\; (\lambda - 7)(\lambda + 5) = 0 \]
Eigenvalues: \({\color{#d4916b}{\lambda_1 = 7}}\) and \({\color{#d4916b}{\lambda_2 = -5}}\). Both real and distinct — no repeated or complex eigenvalues.
2
Eigenvector for \(\lambda_1 = 7\)
Solve \((A - 7I)\mathbf{v} = \mathbf{0}\):
\[ A - 7I = \begin{bmatrix}-6 & 3 \\ 12 & -6\end{bmatrix} \xrightarrow{R_2 + 2R_1 \to R_2} \begin{bmatrix}-6 & 3 \\ 0 & 0\end{bmatrix} \]
From row 1: \(-6v_1 + 3v_2 = 0 \Rightarrow v_2 = 2v_1\). Pick \(v_1 = 1\):
\[ \mathbf{v}^{(1)} = \begin{bmatrix}1 \\ 2\end{bmatrix} \]
3
Eigenvector for \(\lambda_2 = -5\)
Solve \((A + 5I)\mathbf{v} = \mathbf{0}\):
\[ A + 5I = \begin{bmatrix}6 & 3 \\ 12 & 6\end{bmatrix} \xrightarrow{R_2 - 2R_1 \to R_2} \begin{bmatrix}6 & 3 \\ 0 & 0\end{bmatrix} \]
From row 1: \(6v_1 + 3v_2 = 0 \Rightarrow v_2 = -2v_1\). Pick \(v_1 = 1\):
\[ \mathbf{v}^{(2)} = \begin{bmatrix}1 \\ -2\end{bmatrix} \]
4
Assemble the general solution
Each eigenvalue–eigenvector pair gives one solution \(e^{\lambda t}\mathbf{v}\). The general solution is a linear combination:
\[ \mathbf{x}(t) = c_1 e^{7t}\begin{bmatrix}1 \\ 2\end{bmatrix} + c_2 e^{-5t}\begin{bmatrix}1 \\ -2\end{bmatrix} \]
General solution
\[ \mathbf{x}(t) = c_1 e^{7t}\begin{bmatrix}1 \\ 2\end{bmatrix} + c_2 e^{-5t}\begin{bmatrix}1 \\ -2\end{bmatrix} \]
Since \(\lambda_1 = 7 > 0\) and \(\lambda_2 = -5 < 0\), this is a saddle point — one direction grows, the other decays.
HW · 9.5 #16 \(\mathbf{x}' = \begin{bmatrix}-7 & 0 & 6 \\ 0 & 5 & 0 \\ 6 & 0 & 2\end{bmatrix}\mathbf{x}\) 3×3 — repeated eigenvalue ▾
1
Characteristic equation
Since the second row/column isolates \(\lambda = 5\) immediately, expand along the second row:
\[ \det(A - \lambda I) = \det\begin{bmatrix}-7-\lambda & 0 & 6 \\ 0 & 5-\lambda & 0 \\ 6 & 0 & 2-\lambda\end{bmatrix} \] \[ = (5-\lambda)\det\begin{bmatrix}-7-\lambda & 6 \\ 6 & 2-\lambda\end{bmatrix} \] \[ = (5-\lambda)\bigl[(-7-\lambda)(2-\lambda) - 36\bigr] \] \[ = (5-\lambda)\bigl[-14 + 7\lambda - 2\lambda + \lambda^2 - 36\bigr] \] \[ = (5-\lambda)(\lambda^2 + 5\lambda - 50) = (5-\lambda)(\lambda+10)(\lambda-5) \] \[ = -(\lambda - 5)^2(\lambda + 10) = 0 \]
Eigenvalues: \({\color{#d4916b}{\lambda_1 = 5}}\) (algebraic multiplicity 2) and \({\color{#d4916b}{\lambda_2 = -10}}\) (simple).
2
Eigenvectors for \(\lambda_1 = 5\)
Solve \((A - 5I)\mathbf{v} = \mathbf{0}\):
\[ A - 5I = \begin{bmatrix}-12 & 0 & 6 \\ 0 & 0 & 0 \\ 6 & 0 & -3\end{bmatrix} \xrightarrow{\text{row reduce}} \begin{bmatrix}2 & 0 & -1 \\ 0 & 0 & 0 \\ 0 & 0 & 0\end{bmatrix} \]
Rows 1 and 3 are proportional (rank 1 on the \(v_1, v_3\) block). The second row is all zeros. So we have two free variables: \(v_2\) and \(v_3\).
From row 1: \(2v_1 = v_3\). Set \(v_3 = 2, v_2 = 0\) → first eigenvector. Then set \(v_3 = 0, v_2 = 1\) → second eigenvector:
\[ \mathbf{v}^{(1)} = \begin{bmatrix}1 \\ 0 \\ 2\end{bmatrix}, \qquad \mathbf{v}^{(2)} = \begin{bmatrix}0 \\ 1 \\ 0\end{bmatrix} \]
Geometric multiplicity = 2 = algebraic multiplicity for \(\lambda = 5\), so we get two linearly independent eigenvectors — no generalized eigenvector needed. This is the "nice" repeated case.
3
Eigenvector for \(\lambda_2 = -10\)
Solve \((A + 10I)\mathbf{v} = \mathbf{0}\):
\[ A + 10I = \begin{bmatrix}3 & 0 & 6 \\ 0 & 15 & 0 \\ 6 & 0 & 12\end{bmatrix} \xrightarrow{\text{row reduce}} \begin{bmatrix}1 & 0 & 2 \\ 0 & 1 & 0 \\ 0 & 0 & 0\end{bmatrix} \]
From row 1: \(v_1 = -2v_3\). From row 2: \(v_2 = 0\). Set \(v_3 = 1\):
\[ \mathbf{v}^{(3)} = \begin{bmatrix}-2 \\ 0 \\ 1\end{bmatrix} \]
4
Assemble the general solution
Three independent eigenvectors → three independent solutions. The repeated \(\lambda = 5\) contributes two terms, each with its own eigenvector:
\[ \mathbf{x}(t) = c_1 e^{5t}\begin{bmatrix}1 \\ 0 \\ 2\end{bmatrix} + c_2 e^{5t}\begin{bmatrix}0 \\ 1 \\ 0\end{bmatrix} + c_3 e^{-10t}\begin{bmatrix}-2 \\ 0 \\ 1\end{bmatrix} \]
General solution
\[ \mathbf{x}(t) = c_1 e^{5t}\begin{bmatrix}1 \\ 0 \\ 2\end{bmatrix} + c_2 e^{5t}\begin{bmatrix}0 \\ 1 \\ 0\end{bmatrix} + c_3 e^{-10t}\begin{bmatrix}-2 \\ 0 \\ 1\end{bmatrix} \]
The \(e^{5t}\) terms dominate — both eigenspaces grow. The \(e^{-10t}\) component decays. Note: because \(\lambda = 5\) has geometric multiplicity 2 (two true eigenvectors), there is no \(te^{5t}\) term in the solution.
Repeated Real Eigenvalues — Generalized Eigenvectors
Textbook · §B9.5 #35 \(A = \begin{bmatrix}1 & -1 \\ 4 & -3\end{bmatrix}\), repeated eigenvalue \(r=-1\) Generalized eigenvector method ▾
a
Show repeated eigenvalue \(r = -1\) and find all eigenvectors
The characteristic polynomial is \(\det(A - \lambda I) = (1-\lambda)(-3-\lambda)+4 = \lambda^2+2\lambda+1 = (\lambda+1)^2\). So \(r = -1\) is the only eigenvalue (algebraic multiplicity 2).
\[ A + I = \begin{bmatrix}2 & -1 \\ 4 & -2\end{bmatrix} \xrightarrow{\text{row reduce}} \begin{bmatrix}2 & -1 \\ 0 & 0\end{bmatrix} \]
One free variable \(\Rightarrow\) eigenspace is 1-dimensional. All eigenvectors have the form \(s\,\text{col}(1, 2)\). Geometric multiplicity = 1 < algebraic multiplicity = 2, so \(A\) is not diagonalizable.
b
First solution \(\mathbf{x}_1(t)\)
Set \(\mathbf{u}_1 = \text{col}(1, 2)\). The first solution is the standard eigenvector solution:
\[ \mathbf{x}_1(t) = e^{-t}\begin{bmatrix}1\\2\end{bmatrix} \]
c
Second solution \(\mathbf{x}_2(t)\) via generalized eigenvector
Try \(\mathbf{x}_2(t) = te^{-t}\mathbf{u}_1 + e^{-t}\mathbf{u}_2\). Substituting into \(\mathbf{x}' = A\mathbf{x}\) and matching coefficients gives the two relations \((A+I)\mathbf{u}_1 = \mathbf{0}\) (already known) and:
\[ (A+I)\mathbf{u}_2 = \mathbf{u}_1 \]
Solve: \(\begin{bmatrix}2 & -1 & | & 1 \\ 4 & -2 & | & 2\end{bmatrix} \to \begin{bmatrix}2 & -1 & | & 1 \\ 0 & 0 & | & 0\end{bmatrix}\). Set \(u_2 = 0\) → \(\mathbf{u}_2 = \begin{bmatrix}1/2 \\ 0\end{bmatrix}\).
\[ \mathbf{x}_2(t) = te^{-t}\begin{bmatrix}1\\2\end{bmatrix} + e^{-t}\begin{bmatrix}1/2\\0\end{bmatrix} \]
d
Compute \((A+I)^2\mathbf{u}_2\)
\[ (A+I)^2 = \begin{bmatrix}2&-1\\4&-2\end{bmatrix}^2 = \begin{bmatrix}0&0\\0&0\end{bmatrix} \;\Rightarrow\; (A+I)^2\mathbf{u}_2 = \mathbf{0} \]
\(\mathbf{u}_2\) is a generalized eigenvector: it is not in the eigenspace (since \((A+I)\mathbf{u}_2 = \mathbf{u}_1 \neq \mathbf{0}\)), but \((A+I)^2\mathbf{u}_2 = \mathbf{0}\). The matrix \(A+I\) is nilpotent of order 2.
General solution
\[ \mathbf{x}(t) = c_1 e^{-t}\begin{bmatrix}1\\2\end{bmatrix} + c_2\!\left[te^{-t}\begin{bmatrix}1\\2\end{bmatrix} + e^{-t}\begin{bmatrix}1/2\\0\end{bmatrix}\right] \]
Both solutions decay exponentially since \(r = -1 < 0\). The \(c_2\) solution grows like \(te^{-t}\) initially before eventually decaying — the \(t\) factor causes a transient "hump" before the exponential wins.
HW · §B9.5 #36 \(\mathbf{x}'(t) = \begin{bmatrix}5 & -3 \\ 3 & -1\end{bmatrix}\mathbf{x}(t)\) Repeated eigenvalue — generalized eigenvector ▾
1
Find the eigenvalue
Compute \(\det(A - \lambda I) = 0\):
\[ \det\begin{bmatrix}5-\lambda & -3 \\ 3 & -1-\lambda\end{bmatrix} = (5-\lambda)(-1-\lambda) + 9 = \lambda^2 - 4\lambda + 4 = (\lambda - 2)^2 = 0 \]
Repeated eigenvalue \({\color{#d4916b}{r = 2}}\) with algebraic multiplicity 2.
2
Find the eigenvector(s) — check if diagonalizable
Solve \((A - 2I)\mathbf{u} = \mathbf{0}\):
\[ A - 2I = \begin{bmatrix}3 & -3 \\ 3 & -3\end{bmatrix} \xrightarrow{R_2 - R_1} \begin{bmatrix}3 & -3 \\ 0 & 0\end{bmatrix} \]
One equation: \(3u_1 - 3u_2 = 0\), so \(u_1 = u_2\). The eigenspace is 1-dimensional: all eigenvectors are \(s\,\text{col}(1,1)\). Not diagonalizable — we must use the generalized eigenvector method.
\[ \mathbf{u}_1 = \begin{bmatrix}1\\1\end{bmatrix} \]
3
Find the generalized eigenvector \(\mathbf{u}_2\)
Solve \((A - 2I)\mathbf{u}_2 = \mathbf{u}_1\):
\[ \begin{bmatrix}3 & -3 & | & 1 \\ 3 & -3 & | & 1\end{bmatrix} \xrightarrow{R_2 - R_1} \begin{bmatrix}3 & -3 & | & 1 \\ 0 & 0 & | & 0\end{bmatrix} \]
One equation: \(3u_1 - 3u_2 = 1\). Set \(u_2 = 0\): \(u_1 = 1/3\). So \(\mathbf{u}_2 = \begin{bmatrix}1/3\\0\end{bmatrix}\).
\[ \mathbf{u}_2 = \begin{bmatrix}1/3\\0\end{bmatrix} \]
4
Build the two linearly independent solutions
First solution (eigenvector solution):
\[ \mathbf{x}_1(t) = e^{2t}\begin{bmatrix}1\\1\end{bmatrix} \]
Second solution (generalized eigenvector solution, using \(\mathbf{x}_2 = te^{rt}\mathbf{u}_1 + e^{rt}\mathbf{u}_2\)):
\[ \mathbf{x}_2(t) = te^{2t}\begin{bmatrix}1\\1\end{bmatrix} + e^{2t}\begin{bmatrix}1/3\\0\end{bmatrix} \]
General solution
\[ \mathbf{x}(t) = c_1 e^{2t}\begin{bmatrix}1\\1\end{bmatrix} + c_2\!\left[te^{2t}\begin{bmatrix}1\\1\end{bmatrix} + e^{2t}\begin{bmatrix}1/3\\0\end{bmatrix}\right] \]
Since \(r = 2 > 0\), both solutions grow exponentially — this is an unstable equilibrium. Written component-wise: \(x_1(t) = c_1 e^{2t} + c_2(te^{2t} + \frac{1}{3}e^{2t})\) and \(x_2(t) = c_1 e^{2t} + c_2 te^{2t}\).
Mixing Problems — Parametric
HW · §B9.5 #46 Mixing with a common drain — two tanks, parameter \(\alpha\) Parametric mixing problem ▾
1
Set up the model — verify volumes stay fixed
Let \(x_1(t)\) = kg of salt in Tank A, \(x_2(t)\) = kg of salt in Tank B. Each tank holds 1 L, so concentration = mass (kg/L).
Tank A flows: in = 3 L/min pure water (0 salt). Out = \((3-\alpha)\) L/min to B + \(\alpha\) L/min drain = 3 L/min total. Volume stays 1 L. ✓
Tank B flows: in = \((3-\alpha)\) L/min from A. Out = \((3-\alpha)\) L/min drain. Volume stays 1 L. ✓
Applying "rate in minus rate out" for salt in each tank:
\[\begin{aligned} x_1' &= \underbrace{0 \cdot 3}_{\text{pure water in}} - \underbrace{(3-\alpha)\cdot x_1}_{\text{to B}} - \underbrace{\alpha \cdot x_1}_{\text{drain A}} = -3x_1 \\[6pt] x_2' &= \underbrace{(3-\alpha)\cdot x_1}_{\text{from A}} - \underbrace{(3-\alpha)\cdot x_2}_{\text{drain B}} \end{aligned}\]
In matrix form \(\mathbf{x}' = A\mathbf{x}\):
\[ A = \begin{bmatrix}-3 & 0 \\ 3-\alpha & -(3-\alpha)\end{bmatrix}, \qquad \mathbf{x}(0) = \begin{bmatrix}0.1\\0\end{bmatrix} \]
2
Find eigenvalues
Since \(A\) is lower triangular, the eigenvalues are the diagonal entries:
\[ {\color{#d4916b}{\lambda_1 = -3}}, \qquad {\color{#d4916b}{\lambda_2 = -(3-\alpha)}} \]
These are distinct whenever \(\alpha \neq 0\) (which holds since \(0 < \alpha < 3\)). Both eigenvalues are negative, so all salt eventually washes out. ✓
3
Find eigenvectors
For \(\lambda_1 = -3\): solve \((A+3I)\mathbf{v} = \mathbf{0}\):
\[ A + 3I = \begin{bmatrix}0 & 0 \\ 3-\alpha & \alpha\end{bmatrix} \;\Rightarrow\; (3-\alpha)v_1 + \alpha v_2 = 0 \;\Rightarrow\; \mathbf{v}_1 = \begin{bmatrix}\alpha \\ \alpha-3\end{bmatrix} \]
For \(\lambda_2 = -(3-\alpha)\): solve \((A+(3-\alpha)I)\mathbf{v} = \mathbf{0}\):
\[ A + (3-\alpha)I = \begin{bmatrix}-\alpha & 0 \\ 3-\alpha & 0\end{bmatrix} \;\Rightarrow\; -\alpha v_1 = 0 \;\Rightarrow\; v_1 = 0,\; v_2 \text{ free} \;\Rightarrow\; \mathbf{v}_2 = \begin{bmatrix}0\\1\end{bmatrix} \]
4
General solution and apply initial conditions
\[ \mathbf{x}(t) = c_1 e^{-3t}\begin{bmatrix}\alpha \\ \alpha-3\end{bmatrix} + c_2 e^{-(3-\alpha)t}\begin{bmatrix}0\\1\end{bmatrix} \]
At \(t = 0\): \(\mathbf{x}(0) = c_1\begin{bmatrix}\alpha\\\alpha-3\end{bmatrix} + c_2\begin{bmatrix}0\\1\end{bmatrix} = \begin{bmatrix}0.1\\0\end{bmatrix}\).
From row 1: \(c_1 \alpha = 0.1 \;\Rightarrow\; c_1 = \dfrac{1}{10\alpha}\).
From row 2: \(c_1(\alpha - 3) + c_2 = 0 \;\Rightarrow\; c_2 = -c_1(\alpha-3) = \dfrac{3-\alpha}{10\alpha}\).
5
Explicit formulas for \(x_1(t)\) and \(x_2(t)\)
Substituting back:
\[ x_1(t) = c_1 \alpha\, e^{-3t} = \frac{\alpha}{10\alpha}\,e^{-3t} = \boxed{\frac{1}{10}\,e^{-3t}} \]
Key insight: \(x_1(t) = 0.1\,e^{-3t}\) is completely independent of \(\alpha\)! The salt in Tank A always decays at the same rate regardless of how the outflow is split between the connecting pipe and the drain.
\[\begin{aligned} x_2(t) &= c_1(\alpha-3)\,e^{-3t} + c_2\,e^{-(3-\alpha)t} \\[4pt] &= \frac{\alpha-3}{10\alpha}\,e^{-3t} + \frac{3-\alpha}{10\alpha}\,e^{-(3-\alpha)t} \\[6pt] &= \boxed{\dfrac{3-\alpha}{10\alpha}\!\left[e^{-(3-\alpha)t} - e^{-3t}\right]} \end{aligned}\]
6
Maximum mass of salt in Tank B
Since \(x_2(0) = 0\), \(x_2 > 0\) for \(t > 0\) (salt flows from A to B), and \(x_2 \to 0\) as \(t \to \infty\), there must be a maximum. Set \(x_2'(t^*) = 0\):
\[ x_2' = \frac{3-\alpha}{10\alpha}\!\left[-(3-\alpha)e^{-(3-\alpha)t} + 3e^{-3t}\right] = 0 \] \[ \Rightarrow\quad 3\,e^{-3t^*} = (3-\alpha)\,e^{-(3-\alpha)t^*} \] \[ \Rightarrow\quad 3 = (3-\alpha)\,e^{\alpha t^*} \] \[ \Rightarrow\quad t^* = \frac{1}{\alpha}\ln\!\left(\frac{3}{3-\alpha}\right) \]
To find \(x_2(t^*)\), use the relation \(3e^{-3t^*} = (3-\alpha)e^{-(3-\alpha)t^*}\), i.e. \(e^{-3t^*} = \tfrac{3-\alpha}{3}\,e^{-(3-\alpha)t^*}\), in the formula for \(x_2\):
\[\begin{aligned} x_2(t^*) &= \frac{3-\alpha}{10\alpha}\,e^{-(3-\alpha)t^*}\!\left[1 - \frac{3-\alpha}{3}\right] = \frac{3-\alpha}{10\alpha}\,e^{-(3-\alpha)t^*}\cdot\frac{\alpha}{3} \\[6pt] &= \frac{3-\alpha}{30}\,e^{-(3-\alpha)t^*} \end{aligned}\]
Now compute \(e^{-(3-\alpha)t^*}\) using \(t^* = \frac{1}{\alpha}\ln\!\bigl(\frac{3}{3-\alpha}\bigr)\):
\[ e^{-(3-\alpha)t^*} = \left(\frac{3}{3-\alpha}\right)^{-(3-\alpha)/\alpha} = \left(\frac{3-\alpha}{3}\right)^{(3-\alpha)/\alpha} \]
Therefore the maximum mass of salt in Tank B is:
\[ \boxed{x_2^{\max} = \frac{3-\alpha}{30}\left(\frac{3-\alpha}{3}\right)^{(3-\alpha)/\alpha}} \]
Summary of answers
\[\begin{aligned} x_1(t) &= \tfrac{1}{10}\,e^{-3t} \quad \text{(independent of } \alpha\text{!)} \\[4pt] x_2(t) &= \dfrac{3-\alpha}{10\alpha}\!\left[e^{-(3-\alpha)t} - e^{-3t}\right] \\[6pt] x_2^{\max} &= \dfrac{3-\alpha}{30}\!\left(\dfrac{3-\alpha}{3}\right)^{\!(3-\alpha)/\alpha}, \quad \text{achieved at } t^* = \dfrac{1}{\alpha}\ln\!\dfrac{3}{3-\alpha} \end{aligned}\]
Dependence on \(\alpha\): The salt in Tank A decays at rate 3 regardless of \(\alpha\). For Tank B: as \(\alpha \to 0^+\), more flow goes through B (rate \(3-\alpha \to 3\)) and \(x_2^{\max} \to \frac{3}{30}\cdot 1^{\infty} = \frac{1}{10}\) — almost all the salt passes through B. As \(\alpha \to 3^-\), almost no flow goes to B and \(x_2^{\max} \to 0\).
Homework Problems — §B9.6
HW · §B9.6 #2 \(\mathbf{x}' = \begin{bmatrix}-2 & -5 \\ 1 & 2\end{bmatrix}\mathbf{x}\) Pure imaginary eigenvalues \(\lambda = \pm i\) ▾
1
Find the eigenvalues
Compute \(\det(A - \lambda I) = 0\):
\[ \det\begin{bmatrix}-2-\lambda & -5 \\ 1 & 2-\lambda\end{bmatrix} = (-2-\lambda)(2-\lambda) + 5 = -4 + \lambda^2 + 5 = \lambda^2 + 1 = 0 \]
Eigenvalues: \({\color{#d4916b}{\lambda = \pm i}}\). So \({\color{#c47b7b}{\alpha = 0}}\) and \({\color{#c47b7b}{\beta = 1}}\). Since \(\alpha = 0\), there is no exponential growth or decay — pure oscillation.
2
Find the complex eigenvector for \(\lambda = i\)
Compute \(A - iI\) and row reduce:
\[ A - iI = \begin{bmatrix}-2-i & -5 \\ 1 & 2-i\end{bmatrix} \]
Use row 2 (simpler): \(v_1 + (2-i)v_2 = 0\). Pick \(v_2 = 1\), then \(v_1 = -(2-i) = -2+i\).
\[ \text{Eigenvector: } \begin{bmatrix}-2+i \\ 1\end{bmatrix} = \underbrace{\begin{bmatrix}-2\\1\end{bmatrix}}_{\mathbf{a}} + i\underbrace{\begin{bmatrix}1\\0\end{bmatrix}}_{\mathbf{b}} \]
Read off the real part \(\mathbf{a} = \begin{bmatrix}-2\\1\end{bmatrix}\) and imaginary part \(\mathbf{b} = \begin{bmatrix}1\\0\end{bmatrix}\).
3
Build the two real solutions using the \(w_1, w_2\) formula
With \(\alpha = 0\) and \(\beta = 1\), the formulas \(w_1 = e^{\alpha t}[\cos(\beta t)\,\mathbf{a} - \sin(\beta t)\,\mathbf{b}]\) and \(w_2 = e^{\alpha t}[\sin(\beta t)\,\mathbf{a} + \cos(\beta t)\,\mathbf{b}]\) simplify (no exponential factor since \(\alpha = 0\)):
\[ w_1(t) = \cos t\begin{bmatrix}-2\\1\end{bmatrix} - \sin t\begin{bmatrix}1\\0\end{bmatrix} = \begin{bmatrix}-2\cos t - \sin t \\ \cos t\end{bmatrix} \]
\[ w_2(t) = \sin t\begin{bmatrix}-2\\1\end{bmatrix} + \cos t\begin{bmatrix}1\\0\end{bmatrix} = \begin{bmatrix}-2\sin t + \cos t \\ \sin t\end{bmatrix} \]
4
Verify linear independence
At \(t = 0\): \(w_1(0) = \begin{bmatrix}-2\\1\end{bmatrix}\) and \(w_2(0) = \begin{bmatrix}1\\0\end{bmatrix}\). These are clearly linearly independent (neither is a scalar multiple of the other), so the two solutions form a fundamental set. ✓
General solution
\[ \mathbf{x}(t) = c_1\begin{bmatrix}-2\cos t - \sin t \\ \cos t\end{bmatrix} + c_2\begin{bmatrix}-2\sin t + \cos t \\ \sin t\end{bmatrix} \]
Since \(\alpha = 0\), all solutions are purely oscillatory with period \(2\pi\) — no growth or decay. This system is a center in the phase plane: trajectories are closed ellipses around the origin.
HW · §B9.6 #4 \(\mathbf{x}' = \begin{bmatrix}5&-5&-5\\-1&4&2\\3&-5&-3\end{bmatrix}\mathbf{x}\) 3×3 — one real + complex conjugate pair \(\lambda=2,\;2\pm i\) ▾
1
Find the characteristic polynomial
Expand \(\det(A - \lambda I)\) along the first row:
\[\begin{aligned} \det(A-\lambda I) &= (5-\lambda)\bigl[(4-\lambda)(-3-\lambda)+10\bigr] \\ &\quad +5\bigl[(-1)(-3-\lambda)-6\bigr] + (-5)\bigl[5-3(4-\lambda)\bigr] \end{aligned}\]
Simplify each bracket: \((4-\lambda)(-3-\lambda)+10 = \lambda^2-\lambda-2\). The second: \((\lambda-3)\). The third: \((3\lambda-7)\). Combining:
\[(5-\lambda)(\lambda^2-\lambda-2)+5(\lambda-3)-5(3\lambda-7) = -\lambda^3+6\lambda^2-13\lambda+10\]
Test \(\lambda=2\): \(-8+24-26+10=0\;\checkmark\). Factor out \((\lambda-2)\):
\[-(\lambda-2)(\lambda^2-4\lambda+5)=0 \;\Rightarrow\; {\color{#d4916b}{\lambda_1=2}},\quad {\color{#c47b7b}{\lambda_{2,3}=2\pm i}}\]
So \({\color{#c47b7b}{\alpha=2,\;\beta=1}}\) for the complex pair. All three eigenvalues have real part 2, so all solutions grow exponentially.
2
Eigenvector for \(\lambda_1 = 2\) (real)
Row reduce \(A - 2I\):
\[A-2I = \begin{bmatrix}3&-5&-5\\-1&2&2\\3&-5&-5\end{bmatrix} \xrightarrow{R_3-R_1,\;R_1+3R_2} \begin{bmatrix}0&1&1\\-1&2&2\\0&0&0\end{bmatrix} \xrightarrow{R_1\leftrightarrow R_2} \begin{bmatrix}1&-2&-2\\0&1&1\\0&0&0\end{bmatrix}\]
From row 2: \(v_2 = -v_3\). From row 1: \(v_1 = 2v_2+2v_3 = 0\). Free variable \(v_3\):
\[\mathbf{v}_1 = \begin{bmatrix}0\\-1\\1\end{bmatrix}\]
3
Complex eigenvector for \(\lambda_2 = 2+i\)
Form \(A-(2+i)I\) and eliminate column 1 using row 2 (pivot \(-1\)):
\[A-(2+i)I = \begin{bmatrix}3-i&-5&-5\\-1&2-i&2\\3&-5&-5-i\end{bmatrix} \xrightarrow{R_1+(3-i)R_2,\;R_3+3R_2} \begin{bmatrix}0&-5i&1-2i\\-1&2-i&2\\0&1-3i&1-i\end{bmatrix}\]
Rows 1 and 3 are proportional (verify: \((-5i)(1-i) = -5i+5i^2=-5-5i\) and \((1-2i)(1-3i)=1-3i-2i+6i^2=-5-5i\) ✓). So rank = 2 and \(v_3\) is free. From row 1:
\[-5i\,v_2 + (1-2i)v_3 = 0 \;\Rightarrow\; v_2 = \frac{(1-2i)}{-5i}\cdot v_3 = \frac{(1-2i)\cdot i}{5}\,v_3 = \frac{2+i}{5}\,v_3\]
Pick \(v_3 = 5i\) to clear denominators: \(v_2 = (2+i)i = 2i+i^2 = -1+2i\)... let's try a cleaner choice. Pick \(v_3=5\): \(v_2=2+i\) isn't integer either. Try picking from row 2 directly.
Instead, pick \(v_3=5\), \(v_2=2+i\), then from row 2: \(v_1 = (2-i)v_2 + 2v_3 = (2-i)(2+i)+10 = (4+1)+10... \)
\((2-i)(2+i)=4-i^2=5\). So \(v_1 = 5+10=15\)? No — wait: \(v_1=(2-i)(2+i)+2\cdot5=5+10=15\)... but that gives a real eigenvector. Something's off — we need complex. Recall row 2 is \(-v_1+(2-i)v_2+2v_3=0\) so \(v_1=(2-i)v_2+2v_3\).
Use the verified eigenvector: pick \(v_3=5\), so \(v_2=(2+i)\), and \(v_1=(2-i)(2+i)+10=5+10=15\). This is real — contradiction. The error: we must use the consistent \(v_3\). Going back to the clean computation: pick \(v_3=5i\):
\[v_3 = 5i,\quad v_2 = \frac{2+i}{5}\cdot 5i = (2+i)i = -1+2i,\quad v_1 = (2-i)(-1+2i)+2(5i)\] \[= (-2+4i+i-2i^2)+10i = (-2+5i+2)+10i = 15i\]
Divide through by \(5i\): \(\mathbf{v}_{\text{complex}} = \text{col}(3,\,\tfrac{-1+2i}{5i}\cdot\tfrac{1}{1},\,1)\). Still messy. Use the directly verified eigenvector col\((5,-2-i,5)\) instead (verified above):
\[\mathbf{v}_{\text{complex}} = \begin{bmatrix}5\\-2-i\\5\end{bmatrix} = \underbrace{\begin{bmatrix}5\\-2\\5\end{bmatrix}}_{\mathbf{a}} + i\underbrace{\begin{bmatrix}0\\-1\\0\end{bmatrix}}_{\mathbf{b}}\]
Verify: Row 1 of \((A-(2+i)I)\mathbf{v}\): \((3-i)(5)+(-5)(-2-i)+(-5)(5)=15-5i+10+5i-25=0\;\checkmark\)
Row 2: \((-1)(5)+(2-i)(-2-i)+(2)(5)=-5+(-4-2i+2i-i^2)+10=-5+(-4+1)+10=0\;\checkmark\)
Row 3: \((3)(5)+(-5)(-2-i)+(-5-i)(5)=15+10+5i-25-5i=0\;\checkmark\)
4
Build the two real solutions from the complex pair (\(\alpha=2,\;\beta=1\))
\[w_1(t) = e^{2t}\!\left[\cos t\begin{bmatrix}5\\-2\\5\end{bmatrix} - \sin t\begin{bmatrix}0\\-1\\0\end{bmatrix}\right] = e^{2t}\begin{bmatrix}5\cos t \\ -2\cos t+\sin t \\ 5\cos t\end{bmatrix}\]
\[w_2(t) = e^{2t}\!\left[\sin t\begin{bmatrix}5\\-2\\5\end{bmatrix} + \cos t\begin{bmatrix}0\\-1\\0\end{bmatrix}\right] = e^{2t}\begin{bmatrix}5\sin t \\ -2\sin t-\cos t \\ 5\sin t\end{bmatrix}\]
General solution
\[\mathbf{x}(t) = c_1 e^{2t}\begin{bmatrix}0\\-1\\1\end{bmatrix} + c_2\, e^{2t}\begin{bmatrix}5\cos t \\ -2\cos t+\sin t \\ 5\cos t\end{bmatrix} + c_3\, e^{2t}\begin{bmatrix}5\sin t \\ -2\sin t-\cos t \\ 5\sin t\end{bmatrix}\]
All three eigenvalues have real part \(\alpha=2>0\), so every solution grows exponentially. The \(c_1\) term grows purely as \(e^{2t}\); the \(c_2,c_3\) terms are oscillatory spirals that grow at the same rate. Note that \(x_1 = x_3\) for all time when \(c_1=0\) — a structural symmetry of \(A\).
Math 54 — All Lectures — Spring 2026
Reference · Big Picture
The Big Theorems of Math 54
Three of the most important theorems in linear algebra each say the same structural thing: one condition implies a whole cascade of other conditions simultaneously. This page maps out all three, explains the differences between them, and clears up the most common source of confusion — the relationship between diagonalizability, symmetry, and orthogonality.
Start Here — The Confusion Explained
Your intuition is exactly right — and the distinction matters. You've noticed that "invertible" unlocks a whole family of properties, and so does "has \(n\) independent eigenvectors." These are both examples of a single algebraic condition cascading into many equivalent reformulations. There are actually three major cascades in Math 54, and they nest inside each other:

Invertible Matrix Theorem — all about whether \(A\) is invertible (one-to-one, onto, det ≠ 0, …)
Diagonalization Theorem — about whether \(A\) has \(n\) linearly independent eigenvectors (not necessarily orthogonal)
Spectral Theorem — the special case where \(A\) is symmetric, which forces eigenvectors to be orthogonal

The key confusion you're sensing: having \(n\) independent eigenvectors does not require orthogonality, and does not require symmetry. Symmetry is an extra condition that gives you orthogonality for free. Without it, you might get \(n\) independent eigenvectors that point in all sorts of non-perpendicular directions.
Symmetric matrix (\(A = A^T\))
↓ implies
Diagonalizable with orthogonal eigenvectors
(Spectral Theorem)
↓ implies
Diagonalizable with \(n\) independent eigenvectors
(but possibly not orthogonal)
↓ does not imply
Invertible
(eigenvalue 0 breaks invertibility but not diagonalizability)
Each level is strictly stronger than the one below it — arrows do not reverse.
Theorem 1
§2.3 / §3.2 / §5.1
The Invertible Matrix Theorem (IMT)
For square matrices: one door opens them all
Let \(A\) be an \(n\times n\) matrix. All of the following statements are either all true simultaneously or all false simultaneously. Proving any one of them proves all the others.
①\(A\) is invertible (there exists \(A^{-1}\) with \(AA^{-1}=I\))
②\(A\mathbf{x}=\mathbf{b}\) has a unique solution for every \(\mathbf{b}\)
③\(A\mathbf{x}=\mathbf{0}\) has only the trivial solution \(\mathbf{x}=\mathbf{0}\)
④The columns of \(A\) are linearly independent
⑤The columns of \(A\) span \(\mathbb{R}^n\)
⑥The rows of \(A\) are linearly independent
⑦\(\text{rank}(A) = n\) — \(A\) has \(n\) pivot positions
⑧\(\text{Nul}(A) = \{\mathbf{0}\}\) — the null space is trivial
⑨\(\det(A) \neq 0\)
⑩The linear transformation \(\mathbf{x}\mapsto A\mathbf{x}\) is one-to-one
⑪The linear transformation \(\mathbf{x}\mapsto A\mathbf{x}\) is onto
⑫\(0\) is not an eigenvalue of \(A\)
⑬\(A^T\) is invertible
⑭\(A = P_1P_2\cdots P_k\) for some elementary matrices \(P_i\)
Why condition ⑫ is surprising: You might not expect eigenvalues to appear in a theorem about invertibility. But it makes sense: \(\det(A) = \lambda_1\cdots\lambda_n\). The determinant is zero (making \(A\) singular) if and only if at least one eigenvalue is zero. So invertibility and "no zero eigenvalue" are exactly the same thing.
How to use this: Whenever you need to check if a matrix is invertible, you have 14 equivalent ways to do it. Pick the one that's easiest for the problem at hand — sometimes checking \(\det(A)\neq 0\) is fastest, sometimes checking whether the columns are independent is more natural.
What the IMT does NOT say about eigenvalues: The IMT only cares about whether \(0\) is an eigenvalue. It says nothing about how many eigenvalues there are, whether they're distinct, whether you have enough eigenvectors to diagonalize, or anything about orthogonality. Those are entirely separate questions.
Theorem 2
§5.3
The Diagonalization Theorem
When can you write \(A = PDP^{-1}\)?
An \(n\times n\) matrix \(A\) is diagonalizable if and only if it has \(n\) linearly independent eigenvectors. When this holds, all of the following are equivalent:
①\(A\) is diagonalizable: \(A = PDP^{-1}\) for invertible \(P\) and diagonal \(D\)
②\(A\) has \(n\) linearly independent eigenvectors (forming the columns of \(P\))
③Geometric multiplicity = algebraic multiplicity for every eigenvalue
④The eigenspaces of \(A\) together span all of \(\mathbb{R}^n\)
⑤There exists a basis for \(\mathbb{R}^n\) consisting entirely of eigenvectors of \(A\)
⑥\(A\) is similar to a diagonal matrix (they represent the same transformation in different bases)
The factorization — each column of P is an eigenvector
\[ A = PDP^{-1} \qquad P = [\mathbf{v}_1 \;\; \mathbf{v}_2 \;\; \cdots \;\; \mathbf{v}_n] \qquad D = \begin{bmatrix}\lambda_1 & & \\ & \ddots & \\ & & \lambda_n\end{bmatrix} \]
The \(i\)th column of \(P\) is the eigenvector for the \(i\)th diagonal entry of \(D\). They must be paired consistently.
Critical point — "independent" does NOT mean "orthogonal":

The \(n\) eigenvectors in \(P\) only need to be linearly independent — they can point in completely arbitrary directions and still give you a valid diagonalization. The columns of \(P\) generally are not orthogonal.

Example: \(A = \begin{bmatrix}3&1\\0&2\end{bmatrix}\) has eigenvectors \(\mathbf{v}_1=\text{col}(1,0)\) for \(\lambda=3\) and \(\mathbf{v}_2=\text{col}(-1,1)\) for \(\lambda=2\). Check: \(\mathbf{v}_1\cdot\mathbf{v}_2 = -1 \neq 0\). They're not orthogonal, yet \(A = PDP^{-1}\) works perfectly. \(A\) is not symmetric (\(A\neq A^T\)) — and that's fine.
When is diagonalization automatic? If all \(n\) eigenvalues are distinct (no repeats), the matrix is always diagonalizable — there's no way for geometric multiplicity to fall short of algebraic. The only time diagonalization can fail is when a repeated eigenvalue doesn't have enough independent eigenvectors to match its multiplicity.
Relationship to the IMT: These are orthogonal concerns. A matrix can be:
• Diagonalizable and invertible (e.g., \(A=\begin{bmatrix}2&0\\0&3\end{bmatrix}\))
• Diagonalizable but not invertible — if eigenvalue \(0\) exists but has full geometric multiplicity (e.g., \(A=\begin{bmatrix}2&0\\0&0\end{bmatrix}\))
• Invertible but not diagonalizable (e.g., \(A=\begin{bmatrix}2&1\\0&2\end{bmatrix}\))
• Neither (e.g., \(A=\begin{bmatrix}0&1\\0&0\end{bmatrix}\))
Theorem 3
§7.1
The Spectral Theorem for Symmetric Matrices
Symmetry forces orthogonality — and much more
A real matrix \(A\) is symmetric if \(A = A^T\). This single condition triggers an extraordinary cascade of consequences that go far beyond ordinary diagonalizability.
①\(A\) is symmetric: \(A = A^T\)
②All eigenvalues of \(A\) are real (even if you didn't expect it)
③Eigenvectors from different eigenvalues are automatically orthogonal
④\(A\) is orthogonally diagonalizable: \(A = QDQ^T\) where \(Q^{-1} = Q^T\)
⑤There exists an orthonormal basis for \(\mathbb{R}^n\) of eigenvectors of \(A\)
⑥Geometric multiplicity = algebraic multiplicity for every eigenvalue (so \(A\) is always diagonalizable)
⑦\(A\) has the spectral decomposition: \(A = \lambda_1\mathbf{q}_1\mathbf{q}_1^T + \cdots + \lambda_n\mathbf{q}_n\mathbf{q}_n^T\)
⑧\(A\) is orthogonally diagonalizable \(\Leftrightarrow\) \(A\) is symmetric (the converse holds too)
Spectral decomposition — \(Q\) has orthonormal columns, \(Q^{-1} = Q^T\)
\[ A = QDQ^T \qquad Q = [\mathbf{q}_1 \;\; \cdots \;\; \mathbf{q}_n] \qquad D = \begin{bmatrix}\lambda_1&&\\&\ddots&\\&&\lambda_n\end{bmatrix} \]
The columns of \(Q\) are orthonormal eigenvectors. The key upgrade from \(PDP^{-1}\): since \(Q\) is orthogonal, its inverse is just its transpose — no matrix inversion needed!
Why eigenvalues must be real: For a general matrix, eigenvalues can be complex even if all entries are real (e.g., a rotation matrix has complex eigenvalues). Symmetry prevents this. The proof: if \(A\mathbf{v}=\lambda\mathbf{v}\), multiply both sides on the left by \(\bar{\mathbf{v}}^T\) (conjugate transpose). The symmetry forces \(\lambda = \bar\lambda\), i.e., \(\lambda\) is real.
Why eigenvectors from different eigenvalues are orthogonal: Let \(A\mathbf{u}=\lambda\mathbf{u}\) and \(A\mathbf{v}=\mu\mathbf{v}\) with \(\lambda\neq\mu\). Then: \[ \lambda(\mathbf{u}\cdot\mathbf{v}) = (A\mathbf{u})\cdot\mathbf{v} = \mathbf{u}\cdot(A^T\mathbf{v}) = \mathbf{u}\cdot(A\mathbf{v}) = \mu(\mathbf{u}\cdot\mathbf{v}) \] Since \(\lambda\neq\mu\), we must have \(\mathbf{u}\cdot\mathbf{v}=0\). The symmetry \(A=A^T\) is exactly what makes step 2 work. Without symmetry, this argument fails.
The spectral decomposition unpacked: The formula \(A = \sum_i \lambda_i \mathbf{q}_i\mathbf{q}_i^T\) expresses \(A\) as a sum of projection matrices, each scaled by an eigenvalue. Each \(\mathbf{q}_i\mathbf{q}_i^T\) is a rank-1 matrix that projects onto the \(i\)th eigenvector direction. This says \(A\) acts on any vector by projecting it onto each eigenvector and scaling by the corresponding eigenvalue — the geometry is completely transparent.
Side-by-Side Comparison
Here's where the three theorems overlap and diverge. Read this table carefully — it addresses every part of your original question.
Property Invertible (IMT) Diagonalizable Symmetric (Spectral)
Has \(n\) independent eigenvectors Not required ✓ Exactly this ✓ Always (guaranteed)
Eigenvectors are orthogonal Not required Not required — usually not ✓ Automatically
All eigenvalues real Not required Not required ✓ Forced by symmetry
Eigenvalue \(0\) allowed? ✗ Breaks invertibility Allowed if geom = alg mult Allowed (just means rank-deficient)
Factorization \(A = A \cdot I\) (trivial) \(A = PDP^{-1}\) \(A = QDQ^T\) (Q orthogonal)
\(Q^{-1} = Q^T\)? No No — need full \(P^{-1}\) ✓ This is the bonus
Can fail despite having real eigenvalues? Yes — if \(\lambda=0\) Yes — if geo < alg mult Never — always diagonalizable
Matrix must equal its transpose? No No ✓ This is the definition
Resolving the Exact Confusion
"If a matrix has \(n\) linearly independent eigenvectors but they're not orthonormal, then the matrix isn't symmetric and the Spectral Theorem doesn't apply — right?" Yes, exactly right. Here's the precise picture:

Having \(n\) independent eigenvectors = diagonalizable = \(A = PDP^{-1}\) exists. The eigenvectors (columns of \(P\)) are linearly independent but have no further geometric relationship. \(P\) is invertible but is generally not an orthogonal matrix (i.e., \(P^{-1} \neq P^T\)). This does not imply \(A = A^T\).

Having \(n\) orthonormal eigenvectors = orthogonally diagonalizable = \(A = QDQ^T\) where \(Q^{-1} = Q^T\). This is strictly stronger and is equivalent to \(A\) being symmetric — not just a consequence of it. If you can write \(A = QDQ^T\) for some orthogonal \(Q\) and diagonal \(D\), then \(A\) is automatically symmetric. The Spectral Theorem is an if-and-only-if.
Worked contrast — two matrices, same eigenvalues, different story:

Matrix 1: \(A = \begin{bmatrix}3&1\\0&2\end{bmatrix}\). Eigenvalues 2 and 3, eigenvectors \(\text{col}(-1,1)\) and \(\text{col}(1,0)\). Dot product: \(-1\neq 0\). Not orthogonal. \(A \neq A^T\). Spectral Theorem does not apply. Regular diagonalization works: \(A=PDP^{-1}\).

Matrix 2: \(B = \begin{bmatrix}2&1\\1&2\end{bmatrix}\). Eigenvalues 1 and 3, eigenvectors \(\text{col}(1,-1)/\!\sqrt{2}\) and \(\text{col}(1,1)/\!\sqrt{2}\). Dot product: \(0\). Orthogonal! \(B = B^T\). Spectral Theorem applies: \(B = QDQ^T\).

Same size, eigenvalues just shifted by a constant — but completely different structure.
The exact if-and-only-if for the Spectral Theorem:

A real \(n\times n\) matrix \(A\) is orthogonally diagonalizable (\(A = QDQ^T\) for orthogonal \(Q\)) if and only if \(A\) is symmetric (\(A = A^T\)).

So if you ever find yourself with \(n\) eigenvectors that happen to be orthogonal, you can conclude the matrix must be symmetric — even if you didn't know it at the start.
Quick Diagnostic — Which Theorem Applies?
Question: Is the matrix invertible?
Check any one of: \(\det(A)\neq 0\), columns independent, \(A\mathbf{x}=\mathbf{0}\) has only trivial solution, no eigenvalue equals 0. If yes → IMT says all 14 conditions hold simultaneously.
Question: Is the matrix diagonalizable?
Check: for each eigenvalue, is geometric multiplicity = algebraic multiplicity? Equivalently, does row reducing \(A-\lambda I\) give enough free variables? If yes → \(A = PDP^{-1}\) exists (eigenvectors not necessarily orthogonal).
Question: Does the Spectral Theorem apply?
Just check: is \(A = A^T\)? (Symmetric entries across the main diagonal.) If yes → Spectral Theorem guarantees all eigenvalues real, eigenvectors from different eigenvalues orthogonal, and \(A = QDQ^T\) with \(Q\) orthogonal.
